  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer8074047518825759703">This rich and moving novel traces the lives of two black heroines from their close-knit childhood in a small Ohio town, through their sharply divergent paths of womanhood, to their ultimate confrontation and reconciliation.<br/><br/>Nel Wright has chosen to stay in the place where she was born, to marry, raise a family, and become a pillar of the black community. Sula Peace has reje</span>
  <span id="freeText8074047518825759703" style="display:none">This rich and moving novel traces the lives of two black heroines from their close-knit childhood in a small Ohio town, through their sharply divergent paths of womanhood, to their ultimate confrontation and reconciliation.<br /><br />Nel Wright has chosen to stay in the place where she was born, to marry, raise a family, and become a pillar of the black community. Sula Peace has rejected the life Nel has embraced, escaping to college, and submerging herself in city life. When she returns to her roots, it is as a rebel and a wanton seductress. Eventually, both women must face the consequences of their choices. Together, they create an unforgettable portrait of what it means and costs to be a black woman in America.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8074047518825759703" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2471421646" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2471421646" />
    <a title="Emily May" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may"><img alt="Emily May" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528390751p2/4622890.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2471421646?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily May" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily May" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may">Emily May</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400033430" href="/book/show/11347.Sula">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=2018">2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2471421646" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14558243945998123224"><blockquote>
  <i>Because each had discovered years before that they were neither white nor male, and that all freedom and triumph was forbidden to them, they had set about creating something else to be.</i>
</blockquote><br/>4 1/2 stars. I have known for some time that I haven't read enough <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3534.Toni_Morrison' title='Toni Morrison' rel='nofollow noopener'>Toni Morrison</a>. Before <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11346.Sula' title='Sula by Toni Morrison' rel='nofollow noopener'>Sula</a>, I had only read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6149.Beloved' title='Beloved by Toni Morrison' rel='nofollow noopener'>Beloved</a>, which is also a great book. Reading this, I can't understand what took me so long to pick up another.<br/><br/>Toni Morrison's writing is frank and uncompromising. She creates characters who burn with </span>
  <span id="freeText14558243945998123224" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>Because each had discovered years before that they were neither white nor male, and that all freedom and triumph was forbidden to them, they had set about creating something else to be.</i>
</blockquote><br />4 1/2 stars. I have known for some time that I haven't read enough <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3534.Toni_Morrison" title="Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">Toni Morrison</a>. Before <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11346.Sula" title="Sula by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">Sula</a>, I had only read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6149.Beloved" title="Beloved by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">Beloved</a>, which is also a great book. Reading this, I can't understand what took me so long to pick up another.<br /><br />Toni Morrison's writing is frank and uncompromising. She creates characters who burn with an inextinguishable fire, and she does it through a series of carefully-written moments; <b>ugly, heartbreaking scenes that somehow capture a person, a time, a place or an injustice in full.</b><br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11346.Sula" title="Sula by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">Sula</a> is, at times, a strange book; it is about an intense, complicated relationship between two black women - Sula and Nel - from the 1920s to the 1940s. I could tell immediately that it was the kind of book I love: small town politics and gossip, intricate relationship dynamics, and gritty no-holds-barred storytelling.<br /><br />It's a <b>short, hard-hitting story</b> that I would call a bildungsroman if that didn't seem a little trite. But it's essentially about Nel and Sula growing up - surrounded by racism, injustice and segregation - and becoming women, discovering their sexuality in very different ways, and living very different lives. Their close friendship is pulled apart by an act that is at once straight-forward, unforgivable without question, but also complex and multilayered.<br /><br />Sula is a fascinating character. Bold, brash, unlikable in some ways... but it is hard not to feel deeply for her. This is a book that in less than two hundred pages takes you deep into the despair and loneliness of life in the Bottom, and at the centre of this is Sula herself. <br /><br />Morrison is just one of those authors that seems to understand certain aspects of life and human nature better than the rest of us. I can't wait to read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11337.The_Bluest_Eye" title="The Bluest Eye by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">The Bluest Eye</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11334.Song_of_Solomon" title="Song of Solomon by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">Song of Solomon</a>.<br /><br /><a href="http://emilymay.me/" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.facebook.com/thebookgeekreviews" rel="nofollow noopener">Facebook</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/thebookgeek01" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | <a href="http://instagram.com/emleemay" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="https://www.youtube.com/c/EmilyMayTheBookGeek" rel="nofollow noopener">Youtube</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="14558243945998123224" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1902772767" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1902772767" />
    <a title="karen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45618-karen"><img alt="karen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596291582p2/45618.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1902772767?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="karen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="karen" href="/user/show/45618-karen">karen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400033430" href="/book/show/11347.Sula">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=pagehabit">pagehabit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1902772767" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6894171489923306930">thanks for this book.<br/><br/><i>Because each had discovered years before that they were neither white nor male, and that all freedom and triumph was forbidden to them, they had set about creating something else to be.</i><br/><br/>this one gets 4 "please don't hit me again, sula!" stars.<br/><br/>and honestly, for more than half of it, it was leaning towards 5 stars, and not just because of stockholm syndrome.<br/><br/>i have never read toni morrison before. her name was at the top of my "authors i have never read, much to my great persona</span>
  <span id="freeText6894171489923306930" style="display:none">thanks for this book.<br><br><i>Because each had discovered years before that they were neither white nor male, and that all freedom and triumph was forbidden to them, they had set about creating something else to be.</i><br><br>this one gets 4 "please don't hit me again, sula!" stars.<br><br>and honestly, for more than half of it, it was leaning towards 5 stars, and not just because of stockholm syndrome.<br><br>i have never read toni morrison before. her name was at the top of my "authors i have never read, much to my great personal shame" list along with tolstoy, balzac, alice munro, etc. and before this book, my impression of her was that she was a very rigidly literary american author who wrote important books about important themes that were technically masterful, but took themselves very seriously and were probably not much fun to read.<br><br>well.<br><br>that is not the case with this one, at least.<br><br>right from the get-go, i was smitten. it was all the things i loved - it was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/80176.Winesburg__Ohio" title="Winesburg, Ohio by Sherwood Anderson" rel="nofollow noopener">Winesburg, Ohio</a>, it was grit lit, it was smalltown gossip and neighborly scrutiny, it was the ingenuity of the disenfranchised, it was the sun <i>rising like a hot white bitch</i>, and best of all, it was FUN! but, like, <i>my</i> kind of fun, where people get set on fire and playtime ends in a body count. this is v.c. andrews without the incest!<br><br>and now i understand why this book kept injuring me - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11346.Sula" title="Sula by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">Sula</a> does NOT play nice. it is a rough book full of rough things too potent to be contained between the covers of the book itself. or maybe the book was just trying to get my attention because it knew i would like it so much. either way, it was worth the price of a few battle scars marking me like sula herself, whose birthmark gives her face a <i>broken excitement</i>. <br><br>to me, this book was absolute perfection when it was focused on the childhood friendship of sula and nel, but it lost something once they grew up. which is a shame, because the childhood parts were SO GOOD. she writes the intensity of nel and sula's intertwining perfectly:<br><br><i>They never quarreled, those two, the way some girlfriends did over boys, or competed against each other for them. In those days, a compliment to one was a compliment to the other, and cruelty to one was a challenge to the other.</i><br><br>and she captures that transition from girlhood to half-understood sexuality wonderfully: <br><br><i>It was in that summer, the summer of their twelfth year, the summer of the beautiful black boys, that they became skittish, frightened and bold - all at the same time.</i><br><br>although i do have to say, her overreliance on the word "beautiful" as a descriptor for men and boys is grating. eeeevery man is beautiful, which is statistically improbable, and it's also lazy wordsmithing in someone who has proven herself to be much better than that. <br><br>but back to the sexxy bits, because you know i'm not into romance or erotica unless it involves all the hilarious ways a human can copulate with a monster or a tater tot or something like that. but human-on-human gyrations tend to leave me cold. however, while it doesn't involve actual intercourse, her descriptions of sula and nel at twelve, <i>wishbone thin and easy-assed</i>, walking to the ice cream store through the gauntlet of men who are themselves passing the time sitting on stoops watching women walk by, through <i>this valley of eyes chilled by the wind and heated by the embarrassment of appraising stares</i>, knowing and not-knowing their effect, delighted and ashamed all at once, and despite the fact that it's totally gross to call a situation in which men in their twenties up through to elderly gentlemen are ogling twelve-year-old girls "hot," still, there's something here that worked on me the way no fifty shades of story of o has, and it comes from the perspective of the girls themselves, and the mysteries of what they have yet to experience:<br><br><blockquote>It was not really Edna Finch's ice cream that made them brave the stretch of those panther eyes. Years later their own eyes would glaze as they cupped their chins in remembrance of the inchworm smiles, the squatting haunches, the track-rail legs straddling broken chairs. The cream-colored trousers marking with a mere seam the place where the mystery curled. Those smooth vanilla crotches invited them; those lemon-yellow gabardines beckoned to them. <br><br>They moved toward the ice-cream parlor like tightrope walkers, as thrilled by the possibility of a slip as by the maintenance of tension and balance. The least sideways glance, the merest toe stub, could pitch them into those creamy haunches spread wide with welcome. Somewhere beneath all of that daintiness, chambered in all that neatness, lay the thing that clotted their dreams.</blockquote><br><br>so you see why i'm frustrated by her repetition of "beautiful" when she can pull off such superior writing. even her descriptions of nature become erotic, although this passage has more of that b-word gumming up the works:<br><br><i>Then summer came. A summer limp with the weight of blossomed things. Heavy sunflowers weeping over fences; iris curling and browning at the edges far away from their purple hearts; ears of corn letting their auburn hair wind down to their stalks. And the boys. The beautiful, beautiful boys who dotted the landscape like jewels, split the air with their shouts in the field, and thickened the river with their shining wet backs. Even their footsteps left a smell of smoke behind.</i><br><br>very saucy stuff, that. <br><br>so, yeah - i really loved this book. i loved the final third less than the beginning, because i didn't really understand what i was meant to be getting out of the story's turn, but it was still excellent writing, and it closed very nicely, so it's an easy four stars, and immunity granted for all injuries sustained. <br><br>***********************************************<br><br>okay, i finished the book. if it lets me live long enough, i will review it soon.<br><br>***********************************************<br><br>IMPORTANT UPDATE: A SECOND ASSAULT UPON MY PERSON BY THIS BOOK:<br><br>okay, so here's something weird. i started this book yesterday, and read several chapters just before bed. when i woke up, i had this gigantic bruise on my eyelid:<br><br><a href="http://s1131.photobucket.com/user/kettincat/media/flat%20stanley/flat%20stanley003/IMG_9596_zpsbbvwa2c6.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1486335511i/21905286._SY540_.jpg" alt=" photo IMG_9596_zpsbbvwa2c6.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><a href="http://s1131.photobucket.com/user/kettincat/media/flat%20stanley/flat%20stanley003/IMG_9591_zpsho5fuy3p.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1486335511i/21905287._SX540_.jpg" alt=" photo IMG_9591_zpsho5fuy3p.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>i have no memory of any trauma to my eye (and i am eye-attack-phobic, so i'd remember) and i wear my glasses all day, which protects me from such trauma. the only way this could have happened would have been when my glasses were off, while i was asleep. when my glasses were off, while i was asleep, WITH THIS BOOK NEXT TO ME IN BED.<br><br>seriously, sula - what's your beef with me?<br><br>although i gotta say, i like how it makes me look like i'm wearing fancy new wave eyeshadow.<br><br><a href="http://s1131.photobucket.com/user/kettincat/media/flat%20stanley/flat%20stanley003/IMG_9595_zpswxtb9xnl.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1486335511i/21905288._SX540_.jpg" alt=" photo IMG_9595_zpswxtb9xnl.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>***********************************************<br><br>the final book in my <a href="https://pagehabit.com/quarterly/literary-fiction-book-boxes" rel="nofollow noopener">quarterly literary fiction box from pagehabit</a>:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1484688219i/21742231._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>sula….<br><br>here's the story with me and sula. long ago, when i was working at barnes and noble and we hosted the new yorker festival every year, i was in the back room on the fourth floor, gathering books to restock the festival displays. while i was grabbing books from a shelf far above my head with my monkey-arms, a hardcover copy of sula slipped from the stack and its very solid lower spine-corner hit me right in the center of my skull with all the force of gravity and book-malice behind it. naturally, i yelled "FUCK YOU, SULA," and naturally i vowed never to read that book, ever. but then this box-thing happened, and now i have to read it, regardless of the abuse i have suffered at its hands. fortunately, this is a paperback, and it is not as tough as its momma. i remain vigilant - i could still get papercuts, after all…<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1528725630i/25720315.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><a href="http://bloggycomelately.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">come to my blog!</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="6894171489923306930" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_239693892" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/239693892" />
    <a title="Rowena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2990642-rowena"><img alt="Rowena" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499471926p2/2990642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/239693892?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rowena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rowena" href="/user/show/2990642-rowena">Rowena</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394480442" href="/book/show/532757.Sula">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2990642-rowena?shelf=afrocentric">afrocentric</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2990642-rowena?shelf=american-lit">american-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer239693892" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6632612792317247764"><b>"Then summer came. A summer limp with the weight of blossoming things. Heavy sunflowers weeping over fences; iris curling and browning at the edges far away from their purple hearts; ears of corn letting their auburn hair wind down to their stalks. And the boys. The beautiful, beautiful boys who dotted the landscape like jewels, split the air with their shouts in the field, and thickened the river with their shining wet backs. Even their footsteps left a smell of smoke behind."</b>- Toni Morrison, <i>S</i></span>
  <span id="freeText6632612792317247764" style="display:none"><b>"Then summer came. A summer limp with the weight of blossoming things. Heavy sunflowers weeping over fences; iris curling and browning at the edges far away from their purple hearts; ears of corn letting their auburn hair wind down to their stalks. And the boys. The beautiful, beautiful boys who dotted the landscape like jewels, split the air with their shouts in the field, and thickened the river with their shining wet backs. Even their footsteps left a smell of smoke behind."</b>- Toni Morrison, <i>Sula</i><br /><br />This is a captivating book about the friendship between two girls (Sula and Nel) with very different personalities. Despite the fact that Sula is the titular character, we're not introduced to her until halfway through the book. Before that we have the opportunity to discover the poor black community where most of the action will take place, and think more about PTSD in the lives of black American soldiers, while waiting for the central story. In particular, the description of Bottom and how it affects the people who live there sets the stage:<br /><br /> <b>"What was taken by outsiders to be slackness, slovenliness or even generosity was in fact a full recognition of the legitimacy of forces other than good ones. They did not believe doctors could heal—for them, none ever had done so. They did not believe death was accidental—life might be, but death was deliberate. They did not believe Nature was ever askew—only inconvenient. Plague and drought were as “natural” as springtime. If milk could curdle, God knows robins could fall. The purpose of evil was to survive it and they determined (without ever knowing they had made up their minds to do it) to survive floods, white people, tuberculosis, famine and ignorance. They knew anger well but not despair, and they didn’t stone sinners for the same reason they didn’t commit suicide—it was beneath them."</b><br /><br />Because I'm reading Morrison's books in chronological order, and <i>The Bluest Eye</i> was read not too long ago, I was maybe more sensitive to the connections and similarities between the two books. In this book, as in <i>The Bluest Eye</i>, the theme of the two Americas emerges, in particular on the theme of parental love. What does love mean when you are a single black mother of three children, abandoned by your husband and living in a poor, black community? I kept going back to read the passage where Hannah is asking her mother, Eva, whether she had ever loved her, and Eva replied, "You settin' here with your healthy-ass self and ax me did I love you? Them big old eyes in your head would a been two holes full of maggots if I hadn't." And also:<br /><br /><b>"Play? Wasn't nobody playin' in 1895. Just 'cause you got it good now you think it was always this good?"</b><br /><br />This sentiment was so reminiscent of The Bluest Eye where the black mother showed her love to her children in somewhat gruff ways which weren't even recognized as love until those children were older. In a sense I feel they were too busy to focus on love as most of us envision it, focusing all their attention on survival instead. <br /><br />I'm still quite conflicted about Sula, although my opinion of her has softened over the years as I myself have gained more empathy through age and personal experiences. In many ways I sympathize with her; she is smart, a rebel of sorts, doesn't like traditional expectations of women, and is very unconventional. She tries to forge her own life, even gaining the courage to leave Bottom. But something is missing in her and Morrison tells us that Sula "had no center, no speck around which to grow." Despite this Morrison is not judgmental in how she portrays her, and it led me to empathizing with her role as an outsider, living in a small community with a small-town mentality :<br /><br /><b>"In a way, her strangeness, her naivete, her craving for the other half of her equation was the consequence of an idle imagination. Had she paints, or clay, or knew the discipline of the dance, or strings; had she anything to engage her tremendous curiosity and her gift for metaphor, she might have exchanged the relentlessness and preoccupation with whim for an activity that provided her with all she yearned for. And like any artist with no art form, she became dangerous."</b><br /><br />Although Morrison focuses mainly on the lives of black girls and women in her writing, she also spares a thought to black men. She looks at black masculinity, particularly in the kind of environment that constrains the lives and movement of black people, and what that manifests as: <br /><br /><b>"So it was rage, rage and a determination to take on a man's role anyhow that made him press Nel about settling down. He needed some of his appetites filled, some posture of adulthood recognized, but mostly he wanted someone to care about his hurt, to care very deeply. Deep enough to hold him, deep enough to rock him, deep enough to ask, “How you feel? You all right? Want some coffee?” And if he were to be a man, that someone could no longer be his mother. He chose the girl who had always been kind, who had never seemed hell-bent to marry, who made the whole venture seem like his idea, his conquest." </b><br /><br />In the end, I really enjoyed this book more than I did a decade ago when I first read it. And I'm awed by how much Morrison can pack into a novella of this size.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6632612792317247764" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1935575766" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1935575766" />
    <a title="Hannah Greendale" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43377498-hannah-greendale"><img alt="Hannah Greendale" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466436186p2/43377498.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1935575766?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hannah Greendale" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hannah Greendale" href="/user/show/43377498-hannah-greendale">Hannah Greendale</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400033430" href="/book/show/11347.Sula">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43377498-hannah-greendale?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43377498-hannah-greendale?shelf=giveaways">giveaways</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1935575766" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10423835121999266942"><a href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=lfVNFbFUYiA&feature=youtu.be' rel='nofollow noopener'>Click here</a> to watch a video review of this book on my channel, <i>From Beginning to Bookend</i>.<br/><br/>In the hills above the valley town of Medallion, Ohio is a small neighborhood known as the Bottom where black residents form a tight-knit community. They are united in their understanding of discrimination and their experience with racial oppression. The Bottom is home to Nel Wright and Sula Peace, two girls whose friendship is solidified by the burden of a horrendous secret. Once grown, they remain guardian</span>
  <span id="freeText10423835121999266942" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=lfVNFbFUYiA&amp;feature=youtu.be" rel="nofollow noopener">Click here</a> to watch a video review of this book on my channel, <i>From Beginning to Bookend</i>.<br><br>In the hills above the valley town of Medallion, Ohio is a small neighborhood known as the Bottom where black residents form a tight-knit community. They are united in their understanding of discrimination and their experience with racial oppression. The Bottom is home to Nel Wright and Sula Peace, two girls whose friendship is solidified by the burden of a horrendous secret. Once grown, they remain guardians of that secret, but an act of betrayal threatens to terminate their friendship forever. <br><br><i>White people lived on the rich valley floor of that little river town in Ohio, and the blacks populated the hills above it, taking small consolation in the fact that every day they could literally look down on the white folks.</i> <br><br>Though <i>Sula</i> posits to be the story of two women, Nel and Sula don't take center stage until roughly fifty pages into the book. Prior to their time in the limelight, the book reads like a collection of character studies, which provides backstory of family history that lays the foundation for the type of drastically different women Nel and Sula each grow up to be. <br><br>Opulent language is regularly employed to describe the setting and character attributes: <br><br><i>Then summer came. A summer limp with the weight of blossomed things. Heavy sunflowers weeping over fences, iris curling and browning at the edges far away from their purple hearts; ears of corn letting their auburn hair wind down their stalks. And the boys. The beautiful boys who dotted the landscape like jewels, split the air with their shouts in the field, and thickened the river with their shining wet backs.</i> <br><br><i>Her voice trailed, dipped and bowed; she gave a chord of the simplest words. Nobody, but nobody, could say "hey sugar" like Hannah. When he heard it, the man tipped his hat down a little over his eyes, hoisted his trousers and thought about the hollow place at the base of her throat.</i> <br><br>Young Nel is raised in an environment that stifles the glowing qualities of her personality, yet she aspires to be wonderful. <br><br><i>Only with Sula did that quality have free reign, but their friendship was so close, they themselves had difficulty distinguishing one's thoughts from the other's.</i><br><br>As a grown woman, Nel is an accepted figure in the community, content with the status quo and the confines of a life as mother and wife. Young Sula, by stark contrast, enjoys the neatness of Nel's parents' house and finds it a comforting opposite to the dirty, cluttered conditions of her own home where her mother - known around town for being loose with men - adheres to a lax method of parenting. As an adult, Sula challenges the status quo with her anarchistic ways, free of the rules for women established by men, making <i>Sula</i> - first and foremost - a study of an outlaw woman disrupting the harmony of a unified neighborhood and tragically injuring a lifelong friendship. <br><br><i>They said that Sula slept with white men. it may not have been true, but it certainly could have been. She was obviously capable of it. In any case, all minds were closed to her when that word was passed around.</i> <br><br>Towards the end of the book, the story shifts without preamble from a third person to a first person narrative for just a few pages. It's likely this was a strategic move, enacted by the author to emphasize a character's deep sense of betrayal, but the sudden and unexpected shift was initially jarring. Once oriented, the scene does allow for a more intimate experience of betrayal as told through the eyes of a character via a first person narrative. <br><br>Coming full circle, the book concludes nicely by deferring to the characters introduced in its opening pages. <br><br>With only limited time devoted to its two leading characters, <i>Sula</i> is a tragic portrait of a woman breaking societal rules and suffering the grievous consequences of her actions. <br>-<br><br>My deepest gratitude to <a href="https://quarterly.co/" rel="nofollow noopener">Quarterly.co</a> for providing a free <a href="https://quarterly.co/products/literary-box" rel="nofollow noopener">Literary Box</a> with a copy of this book in exchange for an honest review.<br><br>Quarterly.co's Literary Box comes with bookish goodies, a feature book, and two additional books selected by the author of the feature book. <br><br><a href="http://s613.photobucket.com/user/hannahgreendale/media/lit-box1_zpsn9ys4zvc.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1490630586i/22329063.jpg" alt=" photo lit-box1_zpsn9ys4zvc.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>What makes the Literary Box special are the notes written by the author of the feature book. These notes give readers unique insights into the book that only the author would know. <br><br><a href="http://s613.photobucket.com/user/hannahgreendale/media/lit-box2_zpspzzoql4t.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1490630757i/22329073.jpg" alt=" photo lit-box2_zpspzzoql4t.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10423835121999266942" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <a title="Fabian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian"><img alt="Fabian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514061293p2/1036893.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/177318196?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fabian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fabian" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian">Fabian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer177318196" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17795184911618908961">This unerring writer has been the only one to get all 5 star reviews from me so far (for "Beloved," "The Bluest Eye," & this); all of her books have that same wondrous quality. What can be said about our most cherished writer that hasn't already been said? It is really hard to come up with a favorite novel ("Beloved" for its twinges of Goth? "Eye" for its incessant play with tenderness and cruelty? Or this, for its inspiring mix of grief from [the ultraheavy psychological effects of] "Eye" & the</span>
  <span id="freeText17795184911618908961" style="display:none">This unerring writer has been the only one to get all 5 star reviews from me so far (for "Beloved," "The Bluest Eye," &amp; this); all of her books have that same wondrous quality. What can be said about our most cherished writer that hasn't already been said? It is really hard to come up with a favorite novel ("Beloved" for its twinges of Goth? "Eye" for its incessant play with tenderness and cruelty? Or this, for its inspiring mix of grief from [the ultraheavy psychological effects of] "Eye" &amp; the magnificent deus ex machina at the end, a-la "Beloved"?). Better than Faulkner, the scenes we are shown here vary in tone. Morrison's narrator has certain privileges but also decides what not to show us. Sula involves the strong relationship between 2 women, how it can possibly transcend the love for family, the love for love. It is something so completely foreign to me, so delicious, &amp; as bizarre, as, say Cindi Lauper's anthem "Girls Just Wanna Have Fun." <br /><br />What is it that men miss out on?!<br /><br />After "Sula" I am now suddenly &amp; utterly aware that there are certain circles (circular... perhaps a fitting definition for the manner in which the writer displays her never-normal narrative) which I am barred from entering--the feeling of being shown only glimpses of something I WILL NEVER UNDERSTAND is that mystical magical engine which keeps my nose perpetually in novels.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2335482139" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6763300305669739447">Short and tinged with sadness, <i>Sula</i> charts the rise and fall of a friendship between two Black women living in a conservative Midwestern town. The story follows extroverted Sula and quiet Nel as the pair of girls grow up in radically different households but nevertheless form an intense bond with one another that they sustain over the years, only for it to collapse dramatically when Sula betrays Nel’s trust on a whim, leading to disaster for all. Meanwhile, the writer vividly contrasts images an</span>
  <span id="freeText6763300305669739447" style="display:none">Short and tinged with sadness, <i>Sula</i> charts the rise and fall of a friendship between two Black women living in a conservative Midwestern town. The story follows extroverted Sula and quiet Nel as the pair of girls grow up in radically different households but nevertheless form an intense bond with one another that they sustain over the years, only for it to collapse dramatically when Sula betrays Nel’s trust on a whim, leading to disaster for all. Meanwhile, the writer vividly contrasts images and memories of birth and death, violence and marriage, in a way that borders on the grotesque. The tale’s swift, the characterization stark, and the setting vividly drawn, but the work lacks the depth of Morrison’s best novels, like <i>Beloved</i> or <i>Song of Solomon</i>.</span>

<div id="review_1696800711" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1696800711" />
    <a title="Violet wells" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells"><img alt="Violet wells" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442260743p2/4196681.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1696800711?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Violet wells" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Violet wells" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells">Violet wells</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1696800711" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8420169812623111325">This is the first time I’ve ever struggled to review a book I’ve read. Perhaps this relentless English rain is getting to me and addling my brain? Not that Sula was in any way bad. Just that I find my response to it is as mysterious as the book itself. I could say it’s been a while since I read Toni Morrison and my first response was excitement at the reminder of how stunningly she can write a sentence – “Grass stood blade by blade, shocked into separateness by an ice that held for days”. I coul</span>
  <span id="freeText8420169812623111325" style="display:none">This is the first time I’ve ever struggled to review a book I’ve read. Perhaps this relentless English rain is getting to me and addling my brain? Not that Sula was in any way bad. Just that I find my response to it is as mysterious as the book itself. I could say it’s been a while since I read Toni Morrison and my first response was excitement at the reminder of how stunningly she can write a sentence – “Grass stood blade by blade, shocked into separateness by an ice that held for days”. I could say it’s about two girls who strike up a poignant intimacy as children and how one becomes a compromised adult and the other becomes the quintessential outsider until she’s resented and feared by her entire neighbourhood, a neighbourhood that seems to exist on a barren island, cut off from the wider world of opportunity and hope – everyone’s hopes are centred on the rumoured construction of a tunnel and a bridge to the neighbouring town. And how it does a moving job of showing how all the odds are stacked against a black woman living in the USA in the first half of the 20th century – “ because each had discovered years before they were neither white nor male and that all freedom and triumph was forbidden them they set about something else to be." I could say it’s a strange mix of poetic myth and grubby realism with perhaps an absence of narrative drive, of compelling storytelling. Despite the beauty of its language and its moving chronicling of appalling social injustice – “the staggering childish malevolence of their employers”. In fact, I think that’s what I’ll say and leave it at that – except to conclude that in this novel all Morrison’s immense gifts as a writer are on display, except her genius of weaving them all together into riveting storytelling. The ending though is fabulous. 3.5 stars. <br /><br /> The sun's just come out!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8420169812623111325" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1175480994" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1175480994" />
    <a title="Jibran" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran"><img alt="Jibran" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572721231p2/5407952.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1175480994?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jibran" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jibran" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran">Jibran</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/13420445-sula">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=nobel">nobel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1175480994" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5958893839808022179"><i>Hell ain't things lasting forever. Hell is change.</i><br/><br/>It is time for change; slowly, painfully, but inexorably the spirit of the age sheds old rags and dons a new garb. The mutes are beginning to discover a voice that had been trapped in their windpipes; eyes see things that they had hitherto only watched; and hearts ache with a new throb of hope mixed with fear of which no one can tell which is greater. From this sense of foreboding out comes Sula.<br/><br/>The excluded community confined up in the hills out</span>
  <span id="freeText5958893839808022179" style="display:none"><i>Hell ain't things lasting forever. Hell is change.</i><br /><br />It is time for change; slowly, painfully, but inexorably the spirit of the age sheds old rags and dons a new garb. The mutes are beginning to discover a voice that had been trapped in their windpipes; eyes see things that they had hitherto only watched; and hearts ache with a new throb of hope mixed with fear of which no one can tell which is greater. From this sense of foreboding out comes Sula.<br /><br />The excluded community confined up in the hills outside a small Ohio town is made, through centuries of social conditioning, to see themselves as different and separate from the white people. They know who they are and they also know they are not the same as the people who live in the town down the hills. They are <i>different,</i> in every imaginable way. You could <i>see</i> that. <br /><br />They are scandalised when Sula, one of their own, embarks on a path that's opening up out there, a path of education and mobility, of employment and relocation, of mingling with the white folks as their human equal, if not racial, social or political equal. Gods be good, the black people are offered to live their lives like the white folks! <br /><br /><i>“It was a fine cry - loud and long - but it had no bottom and it had no top, just circles and circles of sorrow.”</i><br /><br />When she returns home after a long absence Sula is transformed into an unintelligible mass of thoughts and actions her people find difficult to square: It's like a white girl in black skin. Or so people think. Unpardonable. Outrageous. Her community is devastated; nothing is more sacrilegious than dressing like white people, speaking like them, behaving like them, <i>being</i> like them. And what's more, Sula has taken a white man for a lover. <i>Sula, we're not the same.</i> Ah, what an incredible fact of human psychology that even if you do not lose a sense of identity and self-respect, you eventually come to accept the role to which your oppressor designates you.<br /><br />Sula becomes a pariah in her own community, uncomprehending and incomprehensible. The ominous signs that lend her a preternatural aura testify to something strange. People see those signs in retrospect, from her birth to childhood, from her growing up as a daughter of a woman abandoned by her husband, from the way she looked at them when she was a child, the way she walked and sat, ate and gestured. Sula, they reach on a terrifying conclusion, is not a young black girl but a phantom implanted from a world of shadows. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[This is as close as the author would get to magic realism in this novel <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> She is almost a witch, and if she really is not, she ought to be one.<br /><br />Sula’s character is a symbol (self-contradictory, torn, divided, compartmentalised, unmappable) of the conflict borne of the changing values that had held together isolated, nebulous, inward-looking black communities across the United States in the age of institutionalised racism. Values constructed so carefully over centuries when challenged elicit a response that’s always out of proportion. Sula is a couldn't-care-less woman whose threatening individuality alienates her from her community. For this she is taken to task. Her own dealings with her family and the community bespeak a cruelty she's picked up in the course of her contact with the outer world. She, a black woman, treats her own kith and kin with a shade of contempt with which they had always been treated by the White Others.<br /><br />Her character elicits mixed reactions. Sometimes you want to blame her, sometimes blame her family, sometimes you want to blame the sudden rush of new ideas that has thrown the whole social equation out of balance. <br />Was it the new life among the white folks that turned her against herself? Or was it to do with her troubled early years, living as she did with her mother who had taken to selling sex as the most natural vocation a woman might take when her husband walked out on her, causing a rupture in relations with the community? Or did her people, unable to take her novelty, pushed her to the wall, turned her into an alien in her own skin? <br /><br />What made Sula, Sula? This is a question you'll be grappling with by the end of the novel.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“You been gone too long, Sula.<br /><br />Not too long, but maybe too far.”</i>
</blockquote><br /><br /><br />Originally posted February '15<br />Reworked September '15</span>
  <a data-text-id="5958893839808022179" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1823107586" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1823107586" />
    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565547225p2/11255849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1823107586?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl">Cheryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400033430" href="/book/show/11347.Sula">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=mesmerizing">mesmerizing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=afro-american">afro-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=fav-authors">fav-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=the-psyche">the-psyche</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1823107586" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2897961660179549512"><i>
  <blockquote>She had no center, no speck around which to grow.</blockquote>
</i><br/>I can't start to explain this book or the feeling I get each time a new chapter (numbered according to years) gives me the anxious expectation similar to unwrapping a piece of chocolate from the box of assortments - you never know what you'll get. <br/><br/>I can't accurately explain why this fluidity of language, this mixture of elegant vernacular, this exhilarating and encompassing flow of words forms trails down my spine and envelops me into a warm c</span>
  <span id="freeText2897961660179549512" style="display:none"><i>
  <blockquote>She had no center, no speck around which to grow.</blockquote>
</i><br />I can't start to explain this book or the feeling I get each time a new chapter (numbered according to years) gives me the anxious expectation similar to unwrapping a piece of chocolate from the box of assortments - you never know what you'll get. <br /><br />I can't accurately explain why this fluidity of language, this mixture of elegant vernacular, this exhilarating and encompassing flow of words forms trails down my spine and envelops me into a warm cocoon that somehow makes me feel shielded, somehow makes me feel understood. <br /><br />I can't pinpoint a character who Sula reminds me of, so uniquely peculiar and atypical she is that even though I don't necessarily like her, nor am I drawn to her, I still understand the themes she embodies, her skepticism about the world, her desire to live in her dreams and her disdain for conformity.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>There, in the center of that silence was not eternity but the death of time and a loneliness so profound the word itself had no meaning. For loneliness assumed the absence of other people, and the solitude she found in that desperate terrain had never admitted the possibility of other people.</i>
</blockquote><br />I can't even start to decipher this ornately drawn friendship between Nel and Sula, this sisterhood that is too tightly boarded to enter, and yet still fragile enough to form cracks; a friendship "so close, they themselves had difficulty distinguishing one's thoughts from the other's." <br /><blockquote>
  <i>They were solitary little girls whose loneliness was so profound it intoxicated them and sent them stumbling into Technicolored visions that always included a presence, a someone, who, quite like the dreamer, shared the delight of the dream.</i>
</blockquote><br />I can't articulate the juxtaposed gloom and excitement that riddles the city of Medallion, nor the irony of the city segregated but still atop a hill that protects and shields the rest of the community from nature's doom; can't explain properly, or conjure up fairly, the delectable concoction of lust and betrayal and jealousy and strife that parallels one woman's psychological journey and self-realization. <br /><br />What I can say is that this is a Toni Morrison novel that stands apart in its singularness, a book and author you have to read to understand, a book that surprisingly doesn't appear in many book club reads even though it would make for pretty interesting discussions. This is the fourth novel of my Morrison journey I started a couple of years ago. First <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11337.The_Bluest_Eye" title="The Bluest Eye by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">The Bluest Eye</a>, then <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5198.Paradise" title="Paradise by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">Paradise</a>, and later, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13152998.Home" title="Home by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">Home</a>. And I can't wait to keep exploring.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2897961660179549512" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2325628098" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2325628098" />
    <a title="Glenn Sumi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28854579-glenn-sumi"><img alt="Glenn Sumi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444333567p2/28854579.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2325628098?book_show_action=true">Mar 14, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glenn Sumi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glenn Sumi" href="/user/show/28854579-glenn-sumi">Glenn Sumi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400033430" href="/book/show/11347.Sula">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28854579-glenn-sumi?shelf=nobel-winners">nobel-winners</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2325628098" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7122336088949563537">Toni Morrison’s novels - allusive, poetic, with plots that are carefully, artfully constructed - take work. You can’t read them casually. But they also offer up rich rewards to those with patience. <br/><br/><i>Sula</i>, her second novel (published in 1973), tells the story of two girls who grow up in the 1920s in a Black hillside community called the Bottom in the small town of Medallion, Ohio.<br/><br/>Nel Wright, as her name implies, does everything right, including get married to a nice Black man and raise children; S</span>
  <span id="freeText7122336088949563537" style="display:none">Toni Morrison’s novels - allusive, poetic, with plots that are carefully, artfully constructed - take work. You can’t read them casually. But they also offer up rich rewards to those with patience. <br /><br /><i>Sula</i>, her second novel (published in 1973), tells the story of two girls who grow up in the 1920s in a Black hillside community called the Bottom in the small town of Medallion, Ohio.<br /><br />Nel Wright, as her name implies, does everything right, including get married to a nice Black man and raise children; Sula Peace, who grew up with a non-traditional household overseen by an eccentric, one-legged grandmother, is a rebel with a slightly demonic streak. Later she takes on an attitude towards men that causes the town to shun her.<br /><br />As children, the two girls take part in something that’s shrouded in secrecy and shame. Only in the novel’s final pages will the truth emerge about that incident.<br /><br />As with <i>Beloved</i> and <i>Song Of Solomon</i>, the two other Morrison novels I’ve read, the language is dense and poetic. This is prose that should be read aloud, savoured. In fact, I reread a couple of passages just to admire them and see how they felt in my mouth and sounded to my ears.<br /><br />This might also explain why Morrison’s novels don’t adapt well to the big or little screen. They’re gorgeously written, but they don’t rely much on plot. Morrison, in her omniscient third person POV, gets into many characters’ heads (with one exception, in which we’re given a long, aria-like monologue by Nel), but that doesn’t have much momentum or drive.<br /><br />Did I like this novel? Yes. I really liked the way it was structured, orienting us at the beginning of each chapter with a year, but often taking its time to tell us who or what we were going to read about.<br /><br />I wish the girls’ friendship had been illustrated a bit more at the beginning. We’re mostly told that they had this great friendship; I wanted to see it evolve naturally. <br /><br />But the scenes Morrison does provide - including a sequence in which the girls are being assessed by the town’s men, and one in which they’re being accosted by white boys (boy does Sula ever have a powerful way to escape this situation) - are stunning. They have to be, because Morrison refers to them later.<br /><br />The theme of how women judge each other over sex and men is communicated vividly. And the uneasy social and geographical relationship between Blacks and whites is handled subtly.<br /><br />It’s a novel that gains in power as it progresses. Incidents have to accrue; time has to pass; words have to be spoken; and people have to die before the full weight of the story is felt.<br /><br />But Morrison delivers, and does so with admirable economy. A lesser writer would have padded out the story, made us love these characters sentimentally, but I appreciated her way of letting the reader fill in the gaps. (One exception: I wanted more, even a sentence or two, about Nel’s children.)<br /><br />I look forward to reading <i>The Bluest Eye</i> and <i>Tar Baby</i> - a misfit character with that name is referenced in this book - next.<br /><br />But I know I’ll need an extended, uninterrupted period of time to read and appreciate them.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7122336088949563537" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14457130-whitney-atkinson?shelf=feminism">feminism</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3204302237" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer1212717357576438872">I wish I would've read this book for class or with a group because I felt like I was reading a story with a lot of points on a map that didn't quite connect for me. I loved the storyline of the two characters this rotated around, but the book also focused on the town at large, and those bits got lost on me. I found I wasn't quite sure what this was trying to do, and I get really easily frustrated with myself when that happens and it just leaves a bad taste in my mouth that I wasn't smart enough </span>
  <span id="freeText1212717357576438872" style="display:none">I wish I would've read this book for class or with a group because I felt like I was reading a story with a lot of points on a map that didn't quite connect for me. I loved the storyline of the two characters this rotated around, but the book also focused on the town at large, and those bits got lost on me. I found I wasn't quite sure what this was trying to do, and I get really easily frustrated with myself when that happens and it just leaves a bad taste in my mouth that I wasn't smart enough to figure it out. I ended up skimming the last 20 pages of this because I just didn't have the energy anymore to piece things together. So although Morrison is undoubtedly one of the most talented writers in the world and there were so many great quotes I marked, I wish I would've tackled this book with Sparknotes open or something because it just went a little over my head.</span>

<div id="review_1904915937" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1904915937" />
    <a title="Raul Bimenyimana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43664238-raul-bimenyimana"><img alt="Raul Bimenyimana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466510542p2/43664238.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1904915937?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Raul Bimenyimana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Raul Bimenyimana" href="/user/show/43664238-raul-bimenyimana">Raul Bimenyimana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400033430" href="/book/show/11347.Sula">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43664238-raul-bimenyimana?shelf=african-african-diaspora">african-african-diaspora</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43664238-raul-bimenyimana?shelf=women-writers">women-writers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1904915937" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2002635236262663493">Loved every sentence of this re-read. I remember the first time I read Sula, I went in expecting an innocent and charming story about friendship from the small talk I had heard of the book. Nothing had prepared me for the complexity in the relationship between Sula and Nel, nothing had prepared me for the force that was Sula. I remember how my feelings towards Sula were, at first fascination, then shock, then almost-loathing, later understanding and loving, and finally missing her when all was d</span>
  <span id="freeText2002635236262663493" style="display:none">Loved every sentence of this re-read. I remember the first time I read Sula, I went in expecting an innocent and charming story about friendship from the small talk I had heard of the book. Nothing had prepared me for the complexity in the relationship between Sula and Nel, nothing had prepared me for the force that was Sula. I remember how my feelings towards Sula were, at first fascination, then shock, then almost-loathing, later understanding and loving, and finally missing her when all was done.<br /><br />The story opens with a short description of the setting of the book. The Bottom, a Black neighbourhood in Ohio; the irony in its name, the history of the place, its attractions. The introduction is told in a reflective and nostalgic tone, of a place distant and disappearing and being gentrified. Then the story of the people of the place begins, focus being on Nel and Sula, two friends who love and need each other in various complex ways.<br /><br />Toni Morrison says in her foreword to this book:<br /><i>"Outlaw women are fascinating - not always for their behaviour, but because historically women are seen as disruptive and their status is an illegal one from birth if it is not under the rule of men. In much literature a woman's escape from male rule led to regret, misery, if not disaster. In Sula I wanted to explore the consequences of what that escape might be, on not only a conventional black society, but on female friendship. In 1969, in Queens, snatching liberty seemed compelling. Some of us thrived; some of us died. All of us had a taste."</i><br /><br />Sula is an outlaw. A woman who lives fully for herself, with no apologies for it to the end. One of the stunning bits of dialogue from this book, the final conversation between Nel and Sula, Nel asks her what she has to show for the life she lived, to which Sula responds: "Show? To who? Girl, I got my mind. And what goes in it. Which is to say, I got me." To which Nel remarks, "Lonely, ain't it?" Sula's brilliant reply: "Yes. But my lonely is mine. Now your lonely is somebody else's. Made by somebody else and handed to you. Ain't that something? A secondhand lonely."<br /><br />Re-reading this meant diving into the richness of this book all over again. The wonderful world that Morrison creates with the Bottom and its people, and the beauty of the language.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2002635236262663493" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2392526425" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2392526425" />
    <a title="Reggie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68616867-reggie"><img alt="Reggie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1526413802p2/68616867.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2392526425?book_show_action=true">May 15, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Reggie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Reggie" href="/user/show/68616867-reggie">Reggie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400033430" href="/book/show/11347.Sula">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68616867-reggie?shelf=2019-favorites">2019-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68616867-reggie?shelf=2017-favorites">2017-favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2392526425" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8413058345879744593">Imagine writing a Black feminist novel that precedes the release of seminal Black feminist texts like Black Macho & the Myth of the Superwoman (1978), Aint I a Woman (1981), & Women, Race & Class (1981), among others. Toni Morrison did just that in 1973 with the release of her stellar second novel, Sula.<br/><br/>Although this novel is called Sula, I wouldn't have been surprised if this novel was called the Bottom, which is the neighborhood in the fictional city of Medallion, Ohio that the story takes pla</span>
  <span id="freeText8413058345879744593" style="display:none">Imagine writing a Black feminist novel that precedes the release of seminal Black feminist texts like Black Macho &amp; the Myth of the Superwoman (1978), Aint I a Woman (1981), &amp; Women, Race &amp; Class (1981), among others. Toni Morrison did just that in 1973 with the release of her stellar second novel, Sula.<br /><br />Although this novel is called Sula, I wouldn't have been surprised if this novel was called the Bottom, which is the neighborhood in the fictional city of Medallion, Ohio that the story takes place in.<br /><br />The Bottom, which is home to a fully realized Black world. Where every January 3rd a holiday called National Suicide Day, created by a World War One Vet named Shadrack, is celebrated. Where Eva is the town matriarch. Arguably the most loving &amp; evil woman this town has ever seen. Home to Hannah Peace who will make love to your man in the pantry &amp; wash your dishes afterwards. The Deweys, Tar Baby, Ajax, but most importantly, Sula Peace &amp; Nel Wright.<br /><br />Sula &amp; Nel are two women who show you what happens when at one point in your life you two are rebels. You share a singular &amp; defiant vision that allows the both of you to see through any &amp; everyone in your town. A vision that will always keep y'all home, on the margins. But what happens when one of you who shared that vision betrays it? They become apart of the people y'all used to see through, yet, When you come back to town after an extended absence she looks at you funny, she wants you to explain yourself in ways you never would have had to before? Whose really wrong? The person who stuck to the vision, or the person who conformed?<br /><br />Besides those questions that stem from Sula &amp; Nel, this novel touches on themes of womanhood as a whole, sexuality, love &amp; if there is a right way to perform it, perception, indiviuduality, gentrification, white flight, the disgusting pervasiveness of anti-Black racism, &amp; how religion can uplift your morals while also compromising your potential to defend against those enemies with a lack thereof.<br /><br />I advise you all to read Sula multiple times and when you do, cherish the gems you dig up from each visit. Morrison is far too kind for providing us with them, especially within 174 pages.<br /><br /><br /><br />(Review from my first read back in 2017): <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.instagram.com/p/BUZ8UZMF7-1/?taken-by=reggiereads">https://www.instagram.com/p/BUZ8UZMF7...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="8413058345879744593" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3164664552" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1356504158075864356">The cover flap of <i>Sula</i> describes it as a novel about a friendship between two women. The friendship between Sula and Nel, (starting when they are young girls) is the center of this intense novel, but there are so many other intertwined layers. Morrison has created a portrait of several strong women who survive, with no help from men, in a black Ohio neighborhood from 1919 to 1965. The men are mostly weak, philandering, drunk or mentally ill. <br/><br/>Each chapter is an eloquent masterpiece, encapsulating</span>
  <span id="freeText1356504158075864356" style="display:none">The cover flap of <i>Sula</i> describes it as a novel about a friendship between two women. The friendship between Sula and Nel, (starting when they are young girls) is the center of this intense novel, but there are so many other intertwined layers. Morrison has created a portrait of several strong women who survive, with no help from men, in a black Ohio neighborhood from 1919 to 1965. The men are mostly weak, philandering, drunk or mentally ill. <br /><br />Each chapter is an eloquent masterpiece, encapsulating a period of time and the essence of Helene, Eva, Hannah, Nel or Sula. I listened to almost the entire novel, then picked up the print version and started from the beginning. As much as I like Toni Morrison's velvety voice, her reading was hard to follow at times. This novel could be read again and again and again. Every sentence is a gold mine. A dazzling, rich work of art.</span>
